CHARGED WITH BEING
MURDERER'S ACCOMPLICE
Officials Think They Have One of the
Men Who Killed Frederick. D.
Whitney of Butte.
WALLACE, Idaho.. March SO.â Peter
Bernier was arrested to-day and charged
with complicity in the murder of Freder
ick D. Whitney. Whitney was foreman
of the Helena-Frisco mill on Canyon
Creek. . On the night of December 2, 1897,
he was taken from his room by masked
men, led down the railroad track and
shot. Whitney came here from Butte,
where he had influential relatives. A re
ward of Sl5OO was offered for tho arrest
and conviction of the guilty men and Gov
ernor Steunenberg offered an additional
reward of fIOOO for each guilty man con
victed, but the reward was never claimed.
The general . impression Is that Bernier
was not a member of the mob, but that
he knows much more than he. told on the
witness stand at the inquest. Â¦.*

AUSTRALASIA TO UPHOLD
THE IMPERIAL PRESTIGE
WELLINGTON, N. Z., March 20.â 1n
bidding farewell to-day to the fourth con
tingent of New Zealand troops leaving for
service in South Africa, the Premier, R,
J. Sudden, emphasized the determination
of Australasia to uphold the Imperial
prestige. The country, he said, had 400,

gency arose to send enough men to South
Africa to release the imperial troops to

with the settlement. Lord . Salisbury's

friendly note of the President of the
United States and the announcement that
Great Britain would not assent to the in
dependence of the republics were all Jn
dorsed by New Zealand, which, . the
Premier added, would maintain that posi
tion at any cost.

SAN JACIXTO, March . 20.â The fact
that a mountain has recently fallen
is turning all eyes, scientific as well
as curious, to San Jacinto. This
mountain or spur of the San Jacinto
peak has already been visited by- nun-*
dreds of local people. .The. camera. fiend
is here by dozens, and newspaper corre
spondents drive In dally. To show the i ex
tent of this great natural catastrophe it
is well . to understand that the San"
; Jacinto peak is 11,000 feet above the.
: sea level, and that the fallen spur had
an altitude of "4000 feet until Christmas
day, when the great Christmas temblor,
shattered its moorings, and now it lies, a
fallen giant. 200 feet below the Jagged
peaks it waa torn down. /.
Was It the result of gravitation or was
It a direct _upheaval Â¦ from, the earth's
bowels beneath the huge mass of earth
and granite?
Question for Geologists.
This Is a question for learned geologists
to solve, but in the meantime the mind is
staggered when contemplating the 600 to
1000 acres of chaos, split across with bot
tomless "fissures, small canyons lost en
tirely under millions of tons of earth, and
valleys tipped to the east when, once they
undulated to the west.
It Is not unlike climbing ' the Swiss
mountains to reach . the sunken tract.
Twelve miles from San Jacinto the road
reaches a bridle path or forest rangers'
trail.- There' one's nerves must be strong,
as the trail Is precipitous and as one as
cends the eye gazes down .hundreds of
feet of perpendicular foothills. . _. .
On Sunday the trail was black with peo
ple anxious to see the fallen mountain. It
requires an. hour's hard riding to reach
the mountain's broken rim.' Fully 150 feet'
below He3'an upturned grove of manza
nita trees. Long furrows of fresh earth
attract the attention, and there is: be
held a jumble such as might have existed
during the formative period. A great
slanting side of blue shale on the eastern
edge of the basin indicates plainly where
the mountain shook itself loose ' and
hurled, Itself, at an angle of forty de
grees. Into the space where valleys; and
canyons had been*slnce time immemorial.
Thus the underground explosions, which
occurred after Christmas and which were
heard every few hours for two months at
San Jacinto, In conjunction with strong
seismic tremors, which were always from
the direction of Mount San Jacinto, are
now to a certain. extent explained.

BAKERSFIELD. March 20.-John Shan

the fissures in the earth and finally found
a .vent in this particular mountain and,

â¢"I was of the opinion at first that most
of? the falling in of the mountain occurred
a week after Christmas, but I. am con
vinced now that one of the greatest slides
occurred only about three weeks ago.
When I first saw it the blue shale would
mold together like putty. A week's ex
posure to the sun renders It very brittle.
Mr.. Rouse, who lives near by heard deep
Internal rumblings, but no shock, proving
that it was the explosion of gases and
not the settling of tons of earth that i
caused , the seismic vibrations. There is
no appearance that It was merely a land
slide. It could not have been a landslide
alone. A landslide would have been higher
where it began. â¢ This is .lower at the
eastern edge and where It recently parted
there comes a Jumbled ridge broken with
crevasses. Concerning the constantly re
curring earthquakes felt I think it pos
sible there are other caverns filling up
and the gases are finding a vent. There
are no evidences of an internal volcano
unless the opening of these vents will
allow a collection of more gases. In that
case there may be" more earthquakes."

viewed had theories worth considerable.

All the members of the diplomatic corps
to-day handed in their cards at the resi
dence of the late general.
Up to the appearance of Edhem Pasha,
-with whom rest the laurels of the Greco-
Turkish war of 1897, Ghazi Osman Pasha
was regarded as "the Only" Turkish gen
eral. He was the g-reat reserve force of
the Sultan. Indeed, the retirement in
which he passed the greater part of his
life since the Russo-Turkish war in 1576
was said to be due in a great measure
to the Sultan's habitual suspicious atti
tude toward those who had rendered the
greatest cervices to his throne. Abdul
Hainid, however, from time to time, paid
him marks of regard.
"The hero of Plevna," who Inflicted on
the Russian invaders the greatest check
they suffered In the campaign, was made
chief of the Imperial Guard and Marshal
of the Palace. For a time he held the
position of Governor General of Crete, and
in 157S was Minister of War. when he in
trodure-d many reforms into the Turkish
army.
Three years ago. when the Greeks had
pained a temporary success at Melouna
Pass, in the north of Thessaly. the Sultan,
Impatient with Edhem Pasha, ordered
Osman Pasha to take chief command.
The general, who was then G5. started for
the front, but the rapid development of
Edhem Pasha's plans, leading to the fall
of Pnareala. decided the Sultan to leave
the direction of the campaign in Edhem's
hands and Osman returned to Constan
tinople, where he lived In seclusion to the
time of his death.
Splendid Defense of Plevna.
"1 compliment you on your defense of
Plevna. It was one of the most splendid
military feats In history."
This was the greeting of Grand Duke
Nicholas of Russia to Osman Pasha as
he met him after the surrender.. Prince
Charles of Roumania and General Sko
beloff as warmly acknowledged the gal
lantry of the defense. The Turkish gen
eral had on the flat clay fields created a
fortress which for & time seemed impreg
nable. By the entrenchments he caused
to be thrown up he arrested for four
months the advance of the Russians. In
three pitched battles he overcame the
finest troops of the. Czar's armies.
On July 1. 1876. he inflicted a loss of
SOOu killed and wounded on his assailants.
The next battle cost the Russians 170 offi
cers and 7136 men, and a three days' stub
born assault of Skobeloff. later on, when
the Russian Grenadiers got within 500
yards of the- trenches, but failed to pass
the fire zone, ended in a Russian loss of
from IS.OuQ to 20,000 men.
It was resolved to starve out the army
of Osman, which originally numbered
about 56,<X>0 men, and an army of 120,000
Russians completed the investment. At
length, forty-live days after the close
elege had been established and 144 day^
after his own arrival at Plevna, Osman
determined on a desperate attempt to cut
his way through. The struggle was of the
fiercest sort, but many hours of bayonet
fighting ended in the Turks being driven
back and the "defender of Plevna sur
rendered with the honors of war."
Was a Man of Iron Will.
The title Ghazl (the victorious),- confer
red on Osman by the Sultan, had not been
bestowed on a Turkish subject for nearly
three hundred years.
In his personal appearance. , though not
much above middle stature, Osman Pasha
waÂ« of athletic build, with a superb head.

Earthquakes? have! been continuous ever

race, at least on the American continent.

RED CDATED
BULLY SCORED
BY DR. JORDAN
Aristocracy, Militarism and
Imperialism De
nounced.
Only Bace Degeneration Ever Known
Is That Due to One or All of
the Above-Named
Causes.
NEW YORK. March 20.â Dr. David
Starr Jordan, president of the Leland
Stanford University, was the speaker at
a meeting of the Patria Club held in the
banquet hall of the Hotel Savoy to-night.
His subject was ."The Blood of the Na
tion." Dr. Jordan began by saying- that
It is recognized that the blood of a nation
in a large degree determines its history,
and that, in a similar fashion, the his
tory of a nation determines its blood. He
said In part:
The only way in -which any rac^ as a whole
has improved has been through the preservation
of its beet and the loss of Us worst examples.
The condition which favors this 1Â« democracy,
equality before the la-w, or the condition which
equalizes opportunity and gives to each man the
rlcrht to stand or fall on the powers God has
tlven him.
The only race degeneration ever known is
that which Is produced by one or all of de
mocracy's arch enemiesâ slavery, aristocracy,
militarism and imperialismâ the four tyrants
of human politics, not one of which appears
without the others.
Degeneracy of the Individual is quite another
thins and has Its own series of causes. . A. lot
of crazy painters, drunken musicians, maudlin
poets and sensation hunter* on the boulevards
prove nothing as to race deeeneracy. Any
man of any race may. degenerate In an envir
onment of vice, disease and absinthe, but he
may leave his race all the cleaner for his de
generation.
War may seem to make men strong when
the hot passions are on, but hot passion is not
inherited, least of all when the warrior is slain
and leaves no Inheritance. War can only waste
and corrupt. If any war is {rood civil war
must be best. The virtues of victory and the
lessons of defeat would be kept within the na
tion. It would protect the nation from the
temptation to flgrht for gold or trade. The rem
edy for most Ills of men Is not to be sought
In war, not in whirlwinds of rebellion that
shake the world, but In peace and Justice,
equality amonsr men and the/ cultivation of
those virtues we call Christian, because they
have been virtues ever since man and society
beran and will be virtues still when the era
of strife Is past and the redcoat bully In his
boots no longer hides the march of man from

Professor Davidson's Views.
Professor George Davidson Is familiar
with the general characteristics of the
San Jacinto- Mountain. He said yester
day that he would not like to enter upon
a discussion of the . happenings recorded
as > taking place there/because he did not
really know any of the facts by personal
observation. Evidently there had been a
disturbance on a large scale. San Jacinto
Mountain was 10,987 feet high. He was
once advised to go upon the mountain to
make observations, . but he considered the
mountain too steep to make it possible to
carry a large telescope - to the summit,
consequently he located for his observa
tions upon . "old Grayback" Mountain,
which is twenty-four - miles distant.
The steepness Â¦ of, San ' Jacinto I Mountain
has a bearing In considering the recently
reported disturbance. It was conceiv
able. Prof essor . Davidson said, that a
huge landslide | should have swept down
a vast mass of matter from the peak. He
had seen the path of a landslide in Alaska
that was an eighth of . a mile wide and
half a mile long. It had uncovered solid
rocks and left them bare. "...-' ;i . :â¢ .
'Concerning the -earthquakes he said
that he,, of course, knew nothing. Nor
could he explain the report about the
igneous rock. It would take a mineralo
gist and geologist to speak with authority
as to whether the rock mentioned were
really lgenous. The San Gorgonlo Pass,
he .said, t lies between San Bernardino
Mountain and San Jacinto Mountain. The

Eighty-Six Members of Parliament
Will Labor Together in Erin's
... .. Cause.
LONDON*. March 20.â At a Nationalist
banquet at the Hotel Cecil in London this
evening John- Redmond, leader of the
Nationalist party In the House of Com
mons, who presided, said he regarded the
last nine years of public life In Ireland as
a hideous nightmare.
"Our reunion is sincere," he continued,
"and there !b nothing, humanly speaking,
which the eighty-six- Irish members . of
Parliament cannot obtain from the exi
gencies of the British parties. It is in
credible that English statesmen can be so
blind to the teaching of history as. to
imagine that serious practical grievances
can be mitigated by a royal visit to Ire
land or by a .British celebration of St.
Patrick's day." . â¢ â¢

for South Langford. who proposed the
toast "Ireland," a Nation," was greeted

AWFUL NATURAL PHENOMENON
IN SAN JACINTO MOUNTAINS
He was a Turk among Turksâ taciturn,
energetic, with an iron will. He never
wrote dispatches, his reports to Constan
tinople consisting merely of a few words.
His uniform consisted usually of a jacket
with a broad facing of dark cloth, long
waistcoat of Louis XV style and tight
fltting trousers, with knee bootsâ a dress
presenting a contrast to that of other
Turkish generals. He generally wore a
loose blue cloak, with no apparent mark
on it to designate his rank, and a red
tex: In camp he never wore a sword, but
invariably had a pair of field glasses slung
over his shoulder. From the military
academy at Constantinople he went di
rectly to the Crimean war and rose to an
army corps command in the Servian war.

REGULATES DISPOSAL
OF ESTATES OF ALIENS
Senate Considers a New : Treaty - r Be-
tween - Great' Britain : and the
. United States.
WASHINGTON, March 20.â The Senate
to-day in executive session considered- the
treaty between the United j States and
Great Britain regulating the disposal of
the estates of citizens of one country who
may die in the other country. Senator
Wolcott of the Committee on Foreign Re
lations had charge of the treaty anl ex
plained the legal bearing of the agree
ment, saying that it covered practically,
the same ground- as treaties with other
countries on the 1 same subject. . Senator
Davis, chairman of the Committee.on For
eign Relations, also made a statement In
support of the treaty.' ...
Senator Bacon of the same committee
criticised the treaty as conferring a power
upon the Federal Government In the regu
lation of estates which was an infringe
ment upon the rights of the- States and
Territories." He pointed out that the pond."
ing treaty goes further than any agree
ment with any other power . in- the
fringement of this right, and he especially
criticised the authority granted by tho
treaty to the Queen of England or The
President of the United States to exiend
the provisions . of the treaty to depend
encies. ;%'.:;> â¢
The treaty was amended by striking out
the time limit for ratification, which made
it expire on the 2d inst. No vote was
reached, and -when the. Senate adjourned
there was an Understanding that the
treaty would ng-aln be taken up at 3
o'clock to-morrow.
FAVORABLE REPORT ON
PACIFIC CABLE BILL
Senate Measure Will Provide for Im
mediate Laying of a Line to
. . Honolulu: ' .
.WASHINGTON, March 20.â The Senate
Committee on Naval Affairs to-d*y agreed
to report the bill for a Pacific cable; which
Senator Hale' was. authorized to" t draft at
the last session of the committee* and. the
report was later presented to the Senate.
The bill provides for a present cable only
to Honolulu, but declares a purpose to
extend it in the future, the first. section
reading as follows:' Â¦ - - â¢ Â¦Â¦Â¦Â¦
That to the end of establishing hereof ter
telegraph communication between the 'linit*d
States and the Philippine' lslands and Japan
under the waters of the: Pacific. Ocean, there
shall *be | constructed .under . the supervision of
the Secretary of the Navy a : submarine tele
graphic cable or cables .and -connecting 1 land
llnea from the, city of San Francisco. Cal., to
the city of Honolulu, ' over such route as way
be deemed by the Secretary of the Navy to be
the most practicable..
Three million dollars are appropriated
for the work, and. further surveys aie
authorized to determine "the most practi
cable route. The Secretary is > empowered
to cause plans to be prepared immediately
upon the passage of the act and to employ
naval vessels, if necessary,' in the - con
struction. " The control of the cable when
completed is to be transferred to the Post
master General! ' Â»â¢ .â¢; i -
A new provision in the bill requires that
"the cables, wires and other instruments,

a -nirTCB.TISE]ttENTS.REV. FATHER SEBASTIAN DABOVICH-
AN interesting lecture was delivered last nlffht at the Church of the Ad
vent by the Rev. Father Sebastian Dabovlch of the Russian Cathedral.
The subject of the discourse was. "The Relations Between Eastern and
Western Catholicism and What the Orthodox Church Has Done For
Russia." Seated on the platform with the reverend lecturer were Rt. - Rev.
Bishop Moreland, Episcopal Bishop of Sacramento, by whom he was lntro^
<JucÂ»*J; Rev. Father Parrish, rector of the Church of the Advent: Rev. Father
Radcllffe Rev. Edgar Lion. Rev. Father Bartlett and Rev. Mr. Monges.
In his lecture. Father Dabovlch spoke of the severe-struggle which the
GreÂ«k church has experienced in this country and Alaska. Ho deprecated
the fact that the Apostolic Catholic Church of Rusela had met with so little
interest and be attributed it to the unfavorable Jtcht ln which the Episcopal
and Ancllcan churches were placed, owing to the attitude of the â¢ Roman
church. The first part of the le-Hure was concluded with an earnest hope
that God will some day unite all branches of the Catholic church..
"Russia owes everything to- the orthodox church." said Father Dabovich,
"v.hlch created her government and endowed It with the breath of life. Rus
sia's strength lies in her orthodoxy and it is impossible to separate the idea
of religion from that of country. In preparing for battle whole armies have
prayed and received the sacraments. In the Mongolian Invasion it was the
orthodox faith that sustained and forted the people and finally delivered
Russia frcm her enemies." v "
At the conclusion of the lecture a collection was taken up* tor the joint
benefit oi the new Russian Cathedral and the payment of the debt on the
Church of the Advent. ' ' - '

HORTON'S MAN TARGET
Charfrescf Assault to Murder Against

George W. Horton, an ex-convict, and

bad to admit that they had been unable
to find the friend of Horton's old pal, Mc-
Callum. at whom he fired the three shots
on Minna street, near Fourth, and ais
there was no complaint on file the Judge

â¢VC=? RED ANDERSON. Charles Pc
rr 3 * terson and Albert : Winslow, en-
II gaged ln painting the bilges on
â¢ the- steamer Senator, were se
verely burned yesterday by an unex
pected explosion. They were at work
ln the after hold of the ship, and to
aid them in their' labors they used a
lighted candle. \ Gas generated in the
hold and the men worked on without
knowing its proximity. "Â¦>
Winslow waÃ the first to suffer. ..He
advanced his candle ' into one of the
recesses of the hold and there was a
slight' explosion. The flames ignited
Winslow's shirt, and before he could
stop their, progress he 'was severely
burned on the left arm. This should
have been a warning to Anderson and
Peterson, but they continued their
work, unmindful of tljeir danger.
A few hours after the ' accident to
Winslow the explosion was repeated
through the same cause, . and both
men were terribly burned about the
head and face, and had to be removed
to the Harbor Receiving Hospital fOlf 01
treatment. Dr. Deaa worked on the
men for over an hour In an effort to
rellove their â¢. sufferings. After the
burns on ' their hands, face : and ; neck
had been â¢ dressed and the injured
parts bandaged .the men were allowed
to depart for, their, home at 703^Davis
street. They will not be able "to re
:turn to their work for some weeks, as
their injuries are extremely painful.

WENT ON A SPREE AND
COMMITTED SUICIDE
Mrs. Victor Beuvelet Mixed Carbolic
Add With Wine and Seltzer and
Drank It. :
Mrs. Victor Beuvelet, a married woraon,
21- years old, residing at 515 Kearny street,
committed suicide yesterday morning: by
swallowing the contents of a vial of car
bolic acid. v She had been drinking heavily
for several'days, and had poured the cor
rosive poison into a glas3 containing claret
and seltzer water and swallowed the mix
ture. '-.'Â¦ Â¦ -- Â¦â¢

ceased, told -the Coroner's deputies that
his wife had taken the poison by accident
but the woman's associates ridiculed tha
Idea and said that they, had no doubt that
it was a case of deliberate suicide. The
body was taken to the Morgue and an
autopsy held by Morgue Surgeon Dr.
Thomas B. W. Leland,'. who found that
carbolic acid poisoning had been the cause
of death. -,Â¦-.â¢Â¦Â¦ ,

NEW PASSENGER RATE
FOR VALLEY TRAFFIC
Southern Pacific Does Away With the
Old Twenty-Five-Cent Stock
ton Fare.
Yesterday the Southern Pacific Com
pany Issued a circular to Its agents con
taining orders that will do away with the
discriminating passenger tariff that has
heretofore existed between San Francisco
and points south of Stockton.
The old rates were based on a 25 cent
rate from Stockton to San Francisco
mad* necessary by the steamboat compe
tition. The present rate Is the regular
commission rate and will do away- with
complaints from the merchants on thÂ« line
of road affected by the change. ThÂ« cir
cular follows:
On the 22d lnst. limited first class ratesâ
with the exception ot those to and from
Stockton noted belowâ between San Fran-
Cisco, Stockton and all Intermediates on
main or branch line. 'on one hand, and all
points in California 'south, of. Tracy and
Lathrop, inclusive, on the other, and all
rates based thereon, will be withdrawn
Limited rates now in effect between
Stockton,' Tracy,' Bakersfleld, inclusive
and all intermediates on main or branch
lines, and all rates based thereon, will re
main In force. These rates will be speci
fied ln another circular to be issued im
mediately. . Â¦ ,

TROPIC BIRD IS
IN PORT WITH A
VALUABLE CARGO
Makes a Quick Trip Prom
Tahiti and Brings Lots
of Tidings.
The Tropic Bird sailed Into port yester
day laden with 20.000 kilos of copra, 24,220
cocoanuts, one case of mother of pearl
shells, sixty-one tins of vanilla, six
cases of beans and one ton of coral. It
took the vessel thirty-three days to come
from Tahiti. Nothing of Incident occurred
during the long trip, fair weather being
encountered all the way. ?
There Is every likelihood that the City cf
Papeete and the Tropic Bird will be with
drawn from the South Sea trade, as thÂ«
French mails, which both vessels carried,
have been turned over to the Â¦ Ui'io.T
Steamship Company of New Zealand, and
mails from here will be carried by the
Oceanic Steamship Company. News from
the islands in the far Pacific will now be
scarce and far between. When the Tropic
Blid left Tahiti the bark Coldbear was
ln port.
Joe Humbel Missing.
The parents and the police are searching
everywhere for Joe Humbel. a resident of
Los Angeles.. Humbel lately returned on
one of the transports, on which he was
employed as a deckhand. On his arrival
In port he announced that he would leave
immediately for his home ln Los Angles.
Many days Â¦ have passed, and his parents
are very anxious to learn â¢ his where
abouts. Â¦Â¦â¢.Â¦/Â¦â¢Â¦ â¢
Had a Bad Fall.
George Dreamin while at work on the
transport Columbia yesterday fell Into the
hold and broke his arm and was also con
siderably bruised. His Injuries were at
tended to at the Harbor Hospital by Dr.
Thompson. Dreamln's escape from death
was miraculous. He fell a distance of
eighteen feet and struck on a quantity of
Iron stored ln the hold. ,
Rumor has It that the transport Mis
souri will not be used as a hospital ship
ln future, as too large a sum would,have
to be outlayed to place her In proper
shape, and' it is not considered that she is
worth the expenditure. It Is believed that
the steamer Badger, now at Mare Island,
will be substituted for the Missouri and
will be named Law ton, after the bra vÂ»
general who lost his life In the- Philippines.
Two well-known ships have been pur
chased by leading shipping men of Ham
bury. They arÂ« the Royal Forth, a four
masted bark of 3130 tons, commanded by
Captain Cooper, and the Cambrian Queen,
a bark of 1217 tons.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, MARCH 21, 1900.
PLAGUE SCARE
BRINGS GOLD TO
HEALTH BOARD
â Â¦ Â»
Result of a Conference of
Officials Held in the
Mayor's Rooms.
Â»
Large Force of Paid Inspectors Will
Be Turned Loose in Chinatown
to Keep Away Bad *
Germs.
The odor of bad fish and decomposed
pork stood but a ghost of a chance in
Chinatown yesterday. Nothing but the
pungent fumes arising from chloride of
lime, 'that had been sprinkled in copious
quantities over the thoroughfares of the
Mongolian quarter, giving them the ap
pearance of Washoe Valley after a snow
storm, greeted the olfactory sense. of the
heathen, who has been afraid to die dur
ing the past few weeks lest the health.
officials would feed his carcass to the ani
mals in Kinyoun'smenageries, and who has
spent his time dodging the alleged germs
of the insidious bubonic plague.
The cause of the alteration in the scent
which has characterized the , Mongolian
territory for so many years was the al
leged discovery of suspected cases of bu
bonic plague, incidental to a "touch" on
the city treasury for additional funds,
which was successfully accomplished at a
dark lantern conference of city officials
held yesterday.
The doctors who have been Inspecting
Chinatown for somo time past are un
earthing corpses, whose mortal-ooils have
been ahuXtleU off for some time since.
They have been stowea away for burial
at a period when the autopsy surgeon's
knife is not so likely to penetrate their
yellow hides. The Chinese fear post mor
tem examinations, and they conceal the
bodies of their friends rather than apply
for a burial certificate. When the bodies
are found they are badly decomposed and
highly discolored, auch bodies are now
being autopsied by the health officials and
termed "suspicious cases'." Two cases
were reported Monday, and immediately
there was a little stir and the wires were
laid to "work" the city for an additional
appropriation, \esterday the work went
merrily on. Disiniectants were strewn on
the streets and more rubbish hauled to
smolder and annoy citizens on Pacific and
Dupont streets, and yesterday the appro
priatipn was agreed to.
The most "suspicious" case discovered
was that of Lew Sung Wong, who died on
Onedla place a couple of days ago. Of
ficers were immediately sent to place the
house under . quarantine restrictions and
an autopsy was performed. The Board
of Health fa!led to make the essential in
quiry in this case, which If they had
taken. the pains to do, might have set
their minds at ease and Drought less
alarm. The case under question was at
tended by two reputable city physicians
six days before death and both the-doc
tors are willing to give the cause of de
mise as natural causes. They are Drs.
Fitch and Atterberg of the Oriental Hos
pital, who, according to the Chinese Con
sul and managers of the hospital, say the
man had been sick for weeks.
As a result of a secret conference held
yesterday morning in the Mayor's office
one hundred men, acting as sanitary in
spectors, will be turned loose in China
town to make a thorough and rigid house
to-house investigation with a view to lo
cating any cases of suspected bubonic
plague that may exist there. There were
present at the conference Mayor Pholan.
Auditor Wells. Supervisors Jennings and
Hotaling of the Finance Committee, Dr.
Williamson, chairman of the Board of
Health; Health Officer O'Brien and Chief
of Police Sullivan.
Dr. Williamson made an urgent appeal
for more funds to conduct the sanitary
inspection of Chinatown- and stated that
his course had been impelled owing to
the fact that there was another suspicious
case of plague and further said:
I would suggest the employment of one hun
dred men to inspx-ct Chinatown. To this might
be added fifty jÂ»?llce otfioers, whom Chief Sul
livan could be requested to press into service.
I think that $7500 - would be sufficient for the
purpose. It would be advisable to erect a de
tention hospital within the limits of Chinatown,
where all suspected cases could be Isolated, and
the undertaking parlors should be all centered
In one locality.
Auditor Wells was of the opinion that
the money could be appropriated as a
matter of expediency and the Finance
Committee appearing to be convinced of
the reasonableness of the demand virtual
ly decided to recommend that an addi
tional sum be allowed the Health Board
to prosecute the work in Chinatown. This
will Include a wholesale disinfection of
the district, besides the ferreting out of all
suspicious deaths.
As an additional 'precaution two men
wllr be detailed by i the Board of Public
Works and one from the Health Depart
ment to erect screens ln the Chinatown
sewers, which sewers will be flushed and
all dead rats gathered up and burned, as
the rodents are carriers of the germs.
The Board of Public Works will be asked
to co-operate with the Health Board in
securing the men to act as sanitary in
spectors and a meeting, for that parpose
will probably be held to-lay.

AN ABSENT PARTNERâ M. N., City.
If a partner In a business absents himself
from the State and leaves his interest in
the hands of /another and has him act In
his place during his absence, that" Â¦would
not- prevent .the landlord of .the premises
In wnlch the, business is carried on from
raising the rent or giving the occupants
notice to vacate if there is no lease or
verbal agreement. If the party who -was
left In charge had unlimited power of at
torney he could dispose of the Interest he
represents. The fact that the individual
has a chattel mortgage on the absentee's
interest in the store will not authorize
him to sell that interest unless the time
has come to foreclose.
"THE COST OF ADMIRALTY."
AS the days lengthen the death list from Manila
grows ionger. More Americans are sick and
dying there now than six months ago. The
transports come back laden with the dead. Instead
of argosies freighted with commerce, that tie to the
dock and hail the bustling stevedores, the hearse
ships make fast and the hundreds of coffined dead are
lined from the hold and hauled in a long procession
to the Presidio, whence they are sent afar by trains
steamers to their own for sepulture.
It is for them the end. Is it for the country the
beginning? I* the rainbow of oriental trade, arrayed
i:i ail its primary colors, worth all the misery that lies
siient in the thousands of coffins that have traversed
fourteen thousand miles of sea to bring home the
dead? Is that dream of greed worth the vacant-eyed
btare of the maniacs that come back with the dead?
A thousand pairs of handcuffs have gone to Ma
liila to manacle the lunatics whose brains have been
broiled of their wits by a vertical sun. The hearse
ships give up their dead and take on their return
cargo of coffins and handcuffs, for, every day the
tally of the dead and mad grows longer. The official
list for March 19 fills nearly a half column, in fine
type. Eighteen killed and wounded, and thrice
eighteen dead of typhoid, dysentery, beri-beri, small
j.-ox, malarial fevers and other burning pests of the
tropics, and the pitiless sun and poison rains moving
upon the camps with daily growing ardor.
We are asked to see compensation for it all and
forget the incoming cargoes of coffins filled and the
back freight of coffins empty in the victories which
attend our arms in far Luzon, where women fall de
fending their huts with pitiful wooden swords, -and
the country Â«s la;d black and waste and blear as the
crop of coffins it s*er.ds to us. Every battle is a vic
tory for us. and we slay fifty to one, but all the blood
we have spilled puts not one pulse back in the stark
wrists thr.t are quiet in their coffins. Will some one
post and print the gain set over against all this loss?
We have paid every assessment in death. When will
the dividend come? To put it on the sordid ground
of gain, wh'i are the profits expected?
WEDNESDAY MARCH 21, 1900
JOHN D. SFRECKELS Proprietor.

THE SCAVENGER CART NUISANCE.
* FFORTS on the part of the police to abate the
scavenger cart nuisance resulted in the arrest of
* * one can-driver; that is to say, they were futile
md fruitless. There was more than one cart that
tiled to fulfill the requirements of the law, and
cither the police neglected to obey orders, or the
jrders were very indefinite and lax.
The municipal ordinance on the subject say*: "It
shz'ii be unlawful for any person, or persons, firm or
corporation, to use a cart or vehicle for the collection
and removal of general refuse, taken indiscriminately
;.s house refuse and composed of garbage, ashes and
dirt fro:n householders, stores and other places, and
transport the same along and upon the public streets
nnless the same shall be perfectly tight and with a
heavy cover, to Be approved by the Board of- Health,
tightly fastened on said cart or vehicle at all times
when the owner or driver of said cart or other vehicle
iÂ«. not collecting or depositing the general refuse, as
aforesaid, in said cart or vehicle."
Any person who chooses to give attention to the
retuse carts as they pass along the streets will per
ceive they have hardly even a pretenÂ«c of a cover.
The so-called covering is in most cases no more than
cloth, and is neither heavy nor tightly fastened to the
cp.n as the law requires. If the Board of Health have
criven its approval to such covers as these, it has
Ver-n seriously neglectful of its duty. If it have not
jriven approval, then it is still subject to a just charge
of negligence in not setting the police long ago to
the task of arresting the offenders against the ordir
nunce.
The passape through the principal streets of carts
filled with refuse and not securely covered -\% a
frreater menace to the health of the people than any
to-crJlcd "bubonic plague" the Board of Health may
conjure up out of the loathsome diseases of the in
hnbitants of Chinatown. From the uncovered carts
pestilential germs are liable at any time to be blown
about in the atmosphere of the streets thronped with
people. Moreover, even where there are no disease
jrerins. the dust and the odors of the carts constitut*
a nt:is;nre which should be suppressed. It is the
duty of the police to renew its efforts in that direc
tion, and to make them more vigorous than they
were yesterday.

GAGE AND THE PRESS.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CAJLi;, WEDNESDAY, MARCH 21^ 1900.
Â¦ ... â . , .; â¢
NO ONE finds fault with Governor Gage's choice of a time and place to say what he
has to say in defense of his position and practices in California politics. That he
chose the banquet of the Young Men's Republican League at Los Angeles, and
his position as a guest thereat, is a matter of taste for which he alone is responsible.
His explanation of his own acts came in the form of an attack upon the news
papers which exposed those acts. The Governor forgot that he was paying court to
young Republicanism, and that it was no explanation of his personal defects- to loudly
damn the mirror which revealed them. If that were a remedy for physical and moral
blemish, all could become handsome and upright by the simple process of breaking all
the looking-glasses, and repealing all laws that deal with vice and frailty. ;
The Governor assumes that he was attacked and criticized for calling the extra ses^
sion. and thereupon lays about him with all the stallfed parts of speech at the Republican
newspapers, especially the morning papers of San Francisco and Los Angeles: But his
premise is wrong. No paper, that we are aware of, abused him for calling the extra ses
sion. The Call, especially, favored the extra session' and applauided warmly the conclu
sion of its labors in the election of Senator Bard. What has' put the.Governor under the
ban which chafes him is that he alone made the regular session a failure. He alone
brought the party info contempt and near to death by preventing at that* session the
election of a proper man to the Senate/ From the first he dragged his office into the
fight for Burns. There was not an hour from the first ballot to the last that an election
could not have been had if the Governor had confined himself to his legitimate executive
duties and let the Senatorial fight alone. The most unhappy incident of his intrusion
made that session a failure. He put theÃunis fight into everything that occurred in the
session. It was common talk that his veto and his signature to acts passed were con
trolled by the attitude of the authors of bills toward the interest of Burns. He per
mitted an abject devotion to the cause of an unfit and unworthy candidate to influence his
whole official conduct, from his appointments to his action on bills. . Yet after more
than sixty days' effort by all his influence to elect Burns, hej failed. , -
Â» Had this been all it need not have been a matter of great concern. But it was
not all. He had caused a disappointment in one of the great matters for which the Re
publican party had commissioned a Legislature. He had brought in sight a political re
verse in this State. He had alienated an independent, vote by disappointing it. He and
he alone had started that portentous reaction which routed the Republican party in this
city last November. No newspapers had done this. The evil was the result of the candi
dacy of Burns, which lived and moved and had its evil being in the favor of the Gov
ernor and not elsewhere. The press did not do this. The press deprecated it. The press
exposed even- day the filthy politics by which Burns sought to win, and denied party re
sponsibility for it. The press insisted that Burns could not dominate California Repub
licanism and ride it to the devil. The Governor insisted that Burns should mount ' and
ride, no matter what lay at the other end of the road. Had the Governor had his way he
could not have been congratulating the Young Men's Republican League of Los An
geles upon "the election of that stalwart Republican and able gentleman, Thomas R:
Bard, as United States Senator"; for the young men of the league would not have been
banqueting. Burns would have been Senator, and the young men would have been feel
ing about their party as a house does when one of its lady members elopes with a race
track gambler.
Republicans saw a possible retrieval by an extra session and demanded it. The
Call spoke early for it. An attempt was made to turn it to Burns' account, and that effort
failed through the watchfulness of the Republican press. The Governor remained to the
last steadfast for Burns, and would have perverted the extra session if he could. The Re T
publican press prevented it. He made the extra session necessary. The party press
All that is the plain truth of the matter, about which the Governor talked at the
banquet. He went into a fight on the wrong side, and got the worst hiding that has
ever fallen upon the back of a Governor. It ill becomes the dignity of his office to pro
long the exhibition of his stripes and use offensive language in describing the men who
used the "cat" on him. We beg to assure him that they will stand ready to do it again
and again as long as he attempts to do gutter puppolitics in the name of the Republi
can party. He is the party servant, not its master. If he wants to sit in the kitchen
with Dan Burns or keep company with touts, as a personal preference, all right. But he
can't bring them into the parlor, to offend his employer, the Republican party.
NATIONAL INCONSISTENCY.
SIR RICHARD HENN COLLINS, who was
one of the arbitrators in the international
tribunal before which the Venezuelan boundary
dispute was heard, has recently called attention to the
change which the development of events has brought
about in public sentiment among the people of the
United States and of Great Britain.
In order to establish the claims of Venezuela ex-
President Harrison and his associates warmly com
mended the manner in which the Spanish had founded
and built up their colonies. It was argued that the
beneficent character of Spanish colonization was such
that it was to be assumed the claims of the Vene
zuelans were just. The Spaniards, it was said, are 1
considerate people and would never have committed
an aggression or an unfair encroachment upon the
territory of their neighbors, the Dutch colony of
Guiana. On the other hand the British, who, as suc
cessors to the Dutch, were urging their claims against
Venezuela, maintained that the Dutch were the best
and noblest of colonizers, and that it was not pos-'
sible they could have claimed for themselves territory
which justly belonged to their Spanish neighbors. -
That was the way the argument was conducted over
the Venezuelan boundary, but now see what has hap
pened. The Americans, after asserting the bene
ficence of Spanish colonization, have swept it off the
map of the East and West Indies, and the British,
after vehemently asserting the excellence of Dutch
colonies, are now trying to eliminate them from South
Africa. ' .
This change in sentiment may be attributed to the
irony of fate or the whirligig of time, according, as
one views it seriously or humorously. With nation*
as with individuals, circumstances alter cases. We had
a great respect for Spanish colonization so long as it
was Great Britain that was attacking it, but when it
came our turn to take up the white man's burden the
case at once assumed a different aspect. So the Brit
ish were quite sure that all the Dutch colonists did
was quite right so long as the Dutch claims were di
rected against the Spanish, but when they stood in
the way of British expansion, then everything became
different.
The only strange feature of the affair is that the
change should have occurred so promptly and should
have happened to both the Americans and the British
at about the same time. There may be something of
fun in such a "shifting of ground on the part of two
grave and dignified nations, but it is safe to say that
neither the Spanish nor the Dutch perceive the humor
of it. " .
THE GERMAN MEAT BILL.
ONCE more the famous "meat bill" ; supported
by the Agrarians of- Germany has been
"shelved," and this time the shelving occurs
under circumstances that cause no little surprise.
That the measure would meet with strong opposition
in the Reichstag was foreknown, but when it passed
that body the almost universal belief was it had vir
tually become a law, since the Bundesrath is so much
under the control of the landed aristocracy that any
bill designed in their interests hardiy meets with even
a show of opposition there. â¢ ';"\
Reports from Berlin are to the effect that the shelv
ing of the bill is due to the personal, influence of the
Kaiser, who wishes to hold it back until the Reichstag
has voted on the naval programme. If the Agrarians
vote against the naval bill the Kaiser, it is said, will
see to it that the meat'bill is defeated. Thus we are
presented with a view of the august William posing
in the attitude of a political boss using one important
bill as a club with which to force members of a legis
lative body to vote for another. - ,'
, With the fate of the 4 meat bill we are almost as
much concerned as Germany. The object of it is to
shut: foreign meat out of the German market and
that will mean a heavy lossof-trade fo our meat-pro
ducing States. In view of the impending action in
Germany there is already a discussion in the Eastern
States as to what course we should pursue with re
spect to it. The trend of the discussion is in favor
of retaliation of one kind or another. It is said the
German bill will deprive our pork and beef packers
of a trade which amounts to $20,000,000 annually, and
we should by tariff measures shut an equal -amount of
German trade out of the United States..
The Germans now export j large quantities of wine
and beet sugar to the United States, and these are the;
products of the Agrarian landlords who are demand
ing the exclusion of American meats! lard and oleo
margarine from Germany. It would be, therefore,
something like poetic justice, as well as a good eco
nomic retaliation, for vs -to put a prohibitive duty on
such'imports from Germany. Perhaps theconscious
ness that some such action will be taken is one of the
reasons why the meat bill has been /shelved > in the
house of its friends. The landlords of the fiundes
rath are more renowned for ancient lineage and mili
tary swagger than for statesmanship, but they prob
ably know on which side of their bread the "butter is,
and that tariff-making is a game at 'which the United
States can play better and safer than any other nation
on earth. ' Â¦ Â¦

chief tribes sent colonies to all parts of
the Mediterranean and the adjacent
coasts and soon all the mainland on both
sides of the Aegean, as well as the islands,
had Joined in the general chorus of song.
. The three chief tribes all bore their part
In this lyric development, which lasted
some 250 years. But Athens, which was
to be the center of the golden age of
Greek literature, had, so far, taken a
relatively unimportant part in it. E^en
Sparta had shown during the seventh cen
tury a remarkable tendency to foster let
ters. This enlightened policy, so at vari
ance with her usual attitude, waa prob
ably encouraged by Delphi, and though
; not herself productive in literary lines
. Sparta became a great center of talent
from other Greek States. Tyrtaeus, Ter
pander. Thaletas. Alcman. Polymnestus
and others form a remarkable group of
poets and musicians, who must eventual
ly have revolutionized Sparta's attltu'de
toward letters if such enlightened patron
age had continued. But Sparta's attitude
changed completely, doubtless because the
authorities wished definitely to stamp out
a spirit so at variance with the funda
mental tenets of Spartan, discipline, which
taught that the citizen belonged to the
state, that obedience was his first duty,
to become a soldier the object and end of
his existence.
Sparta's renunciation of possible leader
ship in letters left the way clear for Ath
ens, which had been the slowest of all thÂ»
great Greek states to come forward and
play her role in literature. No rreat *ptc
poet had been an Athenian, and In Â«U1 th
lyrlc period only Solon had attained first
rank, and even he was far greater donbt-
Icpb as statesman and patron of letters
than as poet. But now her time bad
come. The expulsion of the tyrants In the
latter part of the sixth century, and more
especially the brilliant leadership of Ath
ens in repelling the Persian Invasions
early In the fifth century, turned all eyes
upon her. Tradition makes Identical the
birth year of Themistocles, the founder of
Athenian naval greatness, and of Aeschy
lus, the father of the Athenian drama.
Under the one Athens was to become tem
porary mistress of the seas; following the
lines marked out by the other she was to
win the hegemony, perhaps for all time.
In letters and art. "With the return of
peace and with enormously Increased
wealth and power and influence, AthÂ»n"*
set to work under wise leaders to makÂ«
herself the real capital of Greece. Wealthy
public men vied with the state In the en
couragement of art and letters. Not only
was native talent encouraged and reward
ed, but from all parts of the Hellenic
world men of gtnius were drawn to the
Attic capital by the stimulus which comes
from contact with the best minds, and by
the rich pecuniary rewards and still richer
compensation in fame , which fell to ex
ceptional merit. It was not that Athens
struck out new lines in literature. There
was nothing distinctly new to be .'done.
Her task was to'perfect in many lines the
beginnings that had already been mads.
If she might be said to have created any
new form it would perhaps be the drama;
but even here she- rather borrowed and
perfected what the Dorians had invented;
The unique thing was that whereas else
where Princes had patronized art and let
ters as in the case of PolycrateÂ«Â» of
Sam'os, the AlÂ»uadae of Thessaly, Hlero
in Sicily, and even at Athens- the Plsia
tratidae, now r whole people of Athens
became encouragers and promoters of
men of genius. It may be doubted If great
talent has ever anywhere else found ?o
stimulating an atmosphere as that of the
Athenian demoa in the fifth century B. C.
The first great literary achievement of
Athens was the drama. The rule still
held that poetry developed before prose.
The suitable medium for the other great
branches of literary composition was still
to be perfected, since history, philosophy
and oratory could not be adequately, ex
pressed except in prose. The lonian
r 'logographers." or . narrators In prose,
had made beginnings in history. , but
these were crude. Hecataeus of Miletus
and Hellanlcus of Mitylenewere clearly
as Inferior to Herodotus in their concep
tion-of history as in the style they com
manded.: The "father of history", belongs
entlrelv to the fifth century, and, though
from Halicarnassus. in Asia Minor, and
using the lonian dialect, owed much to
Athenian Influence. Atany rate, it was
Herodotus who ; fashioned history " into a
new i artistic ; form, and the , lonic dialect
which he used was doubtless not quite the
same as that spoken anywhere, but-fash
ioned -j, for * his purpose Into â¢a - distinctly
literary form. 'His style was called by the
Greek critics the running styleâ where the

THE worthy gentlemen who have been appointed by Mayor Phelan to
choose a flag for San Francisco are expected shortly to make their
flnal selection of a design to be submitted to the Board of Supervisors
for adoption. It was thought that this foolish scheme would fall by
the weight of Its own absurdity, but the Mayor's Indorsement has
given It vitality and It has become serious rather than humorous to the peo
ple of San Francisco. The people of this city already have a flag, and they
want no other banner. They have a standard which represents their highest
and noblest ideals of national and social life. They have' a' flag which binds
them In a union with a great people. It is the flag which means more than
municipal or State life. It has a history and a dignity far surpassing any
other emblem in the minds of citizens.
It Is the flag of Washington and Lincoln. It is the banner which children
are taught to revere in their schoolhouses. It floats over every public build
ing in America and Is sacred -in 'every American home. It Is the flag of the
United States â the stars and stripesâ and It is the only flag that ought to ap
peal to American citizens. The Call offers It to Mayor Phelan as a design
for a flag for San Francisco. His Honor can go further and fare worse in
his selection. > -,
A few American cities have flags, but they shed no dignity upon the
municipalities they represent and they satisfy no need of city life. In some
cities they are objects of derision, as In Chicago, where the emblem of the
windy city is known as the "liver, and lard" flag. â¢ Perhaps Mayor Phelan
would like San Francisco to have the flag of the Phelan. hat. Flags are
needed to distinguish Individuals, tribes and nations. Banners serve no other
purpose. Xo one will say that San Francisco Is a nation, and the city has cer
tainly passed the tribal state. No one, except â¢ Mayor - Phelan perhaps, will
say that San Francisco Is an individual, and his Honor too frequently â¢ In
dulges a habit of introspection to make his judgment valuable.
For what reason, then, does San Francisco need a flag? The heads of gov
ernments, such as an Emperor, a King, a President or a Sultan, have per
sonal flags; but Mayor Phelan Is certainly none of these and can worry
along without a banner. Why not let us have the stars and stripes 'and
change the conditions of the contest? Let the Mayor advertise for sugges
tions for a municipal yell such. as. they have in colleges and universities.
We need a yell more than we do a flag. If the Supervisors, for example,
would open their sessions with an official yell great dignity and impressive
ness would be given to the proceedings. Other departments could do the same
and his Honor the Mayor could have a yell of his own. There Isn't the
slightest 'question: that he has .often felt the need of one, and much would '
be gained by giving It an official character. The Call suggests therefore that
a new competition be started.

TALE BEARERS
MAKE TROUBLE
FOR PAINTERS
Chris Jorgensen Cheerfully
Accepts Decision Relat
ing to Water Colors.
Stanton's Panel Entitled "Evening"
I Was Not Rejected by the Jury
of the Spring Exhibition of
the Art Association.
The San Francisco Art Association an
nounces with pleasure that the spring ex
hibition of works by California artists in
augurating the recently completed Mary
Frances Searles Art Gallery will open to
the public on Friday, March 23. The mem
bers of the association are Invited Â¦to a
prjvate view, and reception on. Thursday
evening. The number of pictures sub
mitted for acceptance greatly exceeded
the original estimate of the management.
When notice was sent. out mvltlng every
artist to submit ten samples of work the
management niust .have fancied .that
many of the painters would not respond
with more than one or two pictures each.
So many pictures, however, were sent "to
the institute* that 100 have been excluded
for-want of wall space. The Jury also
rejected 150 examples on thfe ground that
the work was not up to the! standard.
There is an obvious design in a certain
quarter to provoke dissension and trouble
among the artists and to Involve the Art
Association in an ugly squabble with the
painters. A paragraph In yesterday's Call,
wherein It was announced that Chris Jor
gensen had withdrawn : his water-colors,
was quickly, twisted around to , make It
appear to the public that Mr. Jorgensen
had been ! shabbily treated. The facts in
the case are exactly as -The Call stated.
Mr. Jorgensen ; was not aggrieved by the
decision of the committee. He made no
complaint to any one. He did. not solicit
an interview and > was not interviewed, as
reported. When he ascertained that a
ruling had been made that no water
colors would be exhibited in the main gal
lery .he courteously requested and was
given permission to withdraw his work.
He was disappointed, of course, that his
pictures | could not have the light which
the i new gallery afforded, but -he recog
nized the fairness of the ruling and made
no complaint and manifested no displeas
ure. 'Â¦"' Â¦ V' Â¦'" ' ' "â¢ Â¦Â¦ " * - Â¦Â¦
Some of the talebearers at the. institute
are spreading a rumor that a large panel
painted by John Stanton for the new jinks
room of the Bohemian Club ; was rejected
by the committee appointed to pass upon
the picture presented for the spring exhi
bition of the Art Association. It was Mr.
Stanton's original purpose to exhibit the
panel, entitled "Evening," at the institute,
and permission was obtained to withdraw
the picture from the club, but when the
demand for; space in the new gallery be
came so pressing Robert Howe Fletcher,
curator â¢ of " the â¢ Institute, advised Mr.
Stanton that the panel could not be placed
In proper light without excluding v other
pictures which Â¦ had been accepted. Mr.
Stanton thereupon- readily announced thaf
he ' would : not take the space . from other
exhibitors. By mistake and without ; the
knowledge of .Mr. Stanton the panel was
sent from the club to the Institute.' and as
It was not: placed In the new gallery the
venders of Kossip Immediately Jumped to
the surmise i that -the committee had
"turned-down Stanton.";
separate parts or limbs are, as it were,
disjointedâ as opposed to the periodic In
which the subordinate clauses are grouped
into more comprehensive periods of just
length and proportions. But hla Is the
perfection of a narrative style, and It was
a story of stories that Herodotus had to
tell. First of all, the general subject was
the most thrilling story of Greek an
tiquity, the attempt of the Eastern world
to overwhelm the Western, when Greek
liberty, political Institutions, literature
and art were all at stake. The proper
telling of this great story Involved a clear
exposition not only of what Greece was
and stood for, but of the -character and
motives of her assailant. And properly
to describe Persia it was necessary to
trace the previous history of that great
world power. And so Herodotus' narrative
became really a" history of the world as
the Greeks knew it, for the history of the
Persians led back into that of the Medes;
the contact of the Median empire with
Egypt made necessary a full account of
the country of the Nile; later Persian ex
peditions suggested an account of the
Scythians, as also of the Libyans, and
finally there followed necessarily the
story of lonian A.<ia Minor and the revolt
from Persia, which had prepared the way
for the conflict between Persia and
Greece. Herodotus was a great traveler,
and most of the world which he wrote of
he had himself visited. Those who a few
y_ears ago listened with eager ears to
btanley's account of the country and the
peoples in Africa can have some faint idea
of the interest with which the Greek
world received Herodotus' story of the
great countries beyond the seas. All the
world lovea new stories. Herodotus found
an endle.ss supply of such in connection
with the various peoples of whom he
wrote, and these stories he told in a fash
ion that no historian since has ever had
the art. to equaL And when he came to
tell of Marathon and Thermopylae, Sala
mis and Plataea, even the veterans of
those battles must have felt that the
narration was adequate. The whole re
mains perhaps the most Interesting his
tory in any literatuie.
The next great historian was an Athe
nian of the Athenians. Born about 471,
Thucydides was in his prime when tha
great conflict broke out between the two
foremost states of Greece. He was a pub
lic man of gTeat wealth, an aristocrat,
and connected with the family of the hero
of Marathon, had had the best training
that Athens at her greatest period couM
furnish. He belonged to the best circle*
at Athensâ doubtless to that of the jreat
Pericles himself. He had foreseen from
the beginning the significance of this great
struggle, and he spent twenty years in
collecting the material for its history. His
conception of history was in many re
spects different from that of Herodotus.
Perhaps he- had the other in mind when
he wrote: "Very likely the strictly his
torical character of my narrative may be
disappointing. But if he who desires to
have before his eyes a true picture of tha
events which have happened, ard of thÂ«
like event 3 which may be expected to hap
pen hereafter in the order Of human
things, shall pronounce what I have writ
ten to be useful, then I shall be satisi*ed.
My history .is an everlasting' possession,
not a prize composition which la heard
and forgotten." He regarded the great
struggle between Athens and Sparta,
which lasted twenty-seven years (Wl-KM),
"the greatest evtr known" down to that
time, and he considered It his duty not
simply to narrate the events of the war.
but- to set fortn the causes which led up
to these, and so he became the first philo
sophic historian. He died before his great
work was completed, leaving his account
broken off at 411 B. C; but even thus ajma
of the chief of succeeding historians have
pronounced him the greatest historian of
the world. TbÂ» Earl of Chatham, called
the work "the eternal manual of states
men," and directed that It should oe the
first book which hia son, William Pitt,
should read after entering the university.
Macaulay put the seventh book above
even the "De Corona" of Demosthenes,
as the "ne plus ultra of human composi
tion," and Pericles' great funeral oration
in book two may well be expected to kefcp
its rank as one of the greatest oratorical
compositions in all literature. One passaga
of this oration may properly be quoted
here as setting forth in the words of him
who did most to create the Athens of th.j
fifth century, the spirit that animated the
city and people of Athens at the acme of
the Attic period: "I say that Athens is tho
school of Hellas, and that the individual
Athenian in his own person seems to hava
the power of adapting himself to the most
varied forms of action with the utmost
versatility and grace. This Is no parsing
and idle word, but truth and fact; an.t tho
assertion is verified by the position to
which these qualities have raised thÂ«j
state. For In the hour of trial Athens
alone among her contemporaries la supe
rior to the repott of her. No enemy who
comes against her is indignant at tho re
verses which he sustains at tho hands of:
such a city; no subject complains that hta
masters are- unworthy of him. And wo
shall assuredly not be without witnesses;
there are mighty monuments of our power
which will make us the wonder' of this
and of succeeding ages. We shall not need
the praises of Homer or of any other
panegyrist whose poetry may please for
the moment, although his representation
of the facts will not bear the light of day.
For we have compelled every land and
everj- sea to open a path for our vnlor.
and have everywhere planted eternal me
morials of our friendship and of our en
mity."
Thucydldes* history was completed by
Xenopnon In his Hellenlca; but Xenophon.
however delightful his account of the ex
pedition of the ten thousand with Cyrus
the younger, and however interesting hla
semi-historical romance of Cyrus tha
elder, was no historian. He was a great
writer, but not the legitimate successor
of Herodotus and Thucydldes. The works
of later historians of this period, such as
Ephorus and Theoponipus, have been lost.
Far more than the beginnings of phll
osoohy had been made before the Attlo
period, especially among the lonian.-*.
Prose had been used by Pherecydes of
Svros, Anaxlmander and Anaximenes of
ililetus, Heracllrus of Ephesus and oth
ers but lonic prose was not yet an ade
quate vehicle for. philosophy. Xerwp napes
of Colophon, Parmenldes of Elea and Em
pedocles of Agrigentum chose verse as the
medium of their philosophy, but the true
form for philosophy is not verse, but
prose The great work vu to be dona
here, too. by the Athenians. The great
est Impulse was given to the study of
philosophy by Socrates, who was exactly
contemporary with Thucydides. But hÂ»
left notning In writing, and bis young dis
ciple, Plato, became the great exponent
of his doctrines. But this subject belongs
to a separate study, as does also the ac
count of Athenian oratory, which had m
the Qfth century perhaps Its greatest ret>
resentatlves In Pericles, who left nothing
In writing, and In Antlphon. from whom
fifteen speeches are extant.
To sum up, then: After the drama, ar
tistic prose was the great contribution of
the Attic period in the forma of literature.
The medium which it thuii created is per
haps still unexcelled, as found in the per
fected parts of Thucydldes. In the prin
cipal dialogues of Plato, In the chief
speeches of Demosthenes. The clearness
of perception and the native intellectual
vigor of the Athenians, as well as the nat
ural excellence of the Attic dialect, fitted
them especially for the highest attain
ments in prose literature. But as Greek
poetry had been essentially oral â that Is,
composed to be recited or sung rather
than read â so the excellencies of the best
prose literature were due In large meas
ure to the same cause.

THE GOLDEN AGE OF GRECIAN LITERATURE.
GOLDEN AGES' OF* LITERATURE.
X. THE ATTIC PERIOD.
BT CHARLES FORSTER SMITH. PH. D.
The great creative "period of epic poetry
was practically closed about 800 B. C.
With the development of' individuality
new forms of poetry were required. Out
of -the epic hexameter Greek genius
created in the elegy an instrument not
simply for lament, but which might ex
press philosophic and sententious
thoughts, even political wisdom. .Elegiac
poetry used the dialect and vocabulary of
the epic in the main, and changed the out
ward form only by shortening every other
line into a practical pentameter. The next
step was a greater one. lambic verse
was fashioned Into an Instrument of
satire, and as the verse departed more
from the traditional forms so the vocabu
lary became mpre like that of everyday
life. The new- form was doubtless of
more or less slow growth, but the first
great genius to appropriate it is reputed
its founder, and the Greeks, grateful for
this new literary achievement, hailed
Archilochus as a benefactor second -only
to Homer. But there were other Intimate
and powerful feelings of the heart and
soul still seeking literary expression, and
the 'Aeolians of the island of I,esbos
created melic, or song proper, in various
meters. Passion in all its manifestations,
but especially love, was its burden, and
Alcaeus, Sappho and Anacreon became
its chief representatives.
So far two of the great branches of the
Greek race had been chiefly active in the
development of poetryâ the lonians in
epic, elegiac and iambic poetry, the
Aeolians in lyric proper. The Dorians,
too, were developing a form of lyric suit
ed especially to the worship of the gods
and to the celebration of athletic victories.
These lyrics were to be sung not by in
dividuals, but by carefully trained choirs,
either in procession or with a dance ac
companiment. Of this Dorian choral
lyric, Stesichorus and Arion in earlier
times, Simonldes and Pindar In later, were
the chief representatives. This growth
of the new forms of poetry had been co
incident with the era of colonization in
Greece. ; In the eighth century B. C. the-

Purses came pretty easy for Corrlgan at
Tanforan Park yesterday, his Chimura,

self pocketed behind Gotobed, Red Pirate

through somebody's carelessness it waj

was added, and the chestnut's victory

Espionage for the place. The winner was

NEW YORK. March 20.â There was a bril
liant display of speculative pyrotechnics to
day which was Interesting and informing as
a spectacle, but which must have been at
tended with unpleasant consequfnees for some
one. The speculative outbreak took place in
the New York traction group and was pre
cipitated by the oScial announcement pub
lished this morning that the Metropolitan
Street Railway had secured a controlling In
terest in the Third Avenue Railroad. This at
once confirmed the fears of shorts, which have
been quite acutely manifested for several days,
that there was not a large enough supply of
Third Avenue stock on the market to enable
them to cover their unfilled contracts for de
livery. In other words, the stock was cor
nered. Tha result, briefly etated. was a Jump
of nearly 17 points in the price on top of yes
terday's rise of nearly IS points. The violent
rise in the stock brought out large lots from
holders, wbicb caused many relapses and made
wide and feverish fluctuations all day. But
when the ehorta attempted to cupply their
needs the price always mounted buoyantly
upward again. The New York public utilities
have been the favorite field of operation by
the bears for several days part and the sym
pathetic Htrensth imparted to the group by
the Metropolitan-Third Avenue combination
drove the shorts to cover with a rush. The
extreme advanoes in the group were: Metro
politan. 11%; Brooklyn Transit, 7%; Consoli
dated Gas, 7; Brooklyn Union, a, and Man
hattan, 4iÂ». The possibilities of economy of
operation and elimination of destructive rival
ries as a result of the combination undoubtedly
improves the Ftatus of the Metropolitan and
Third Avenue properties, but the price move
ment of tha day wfis almost wholly dua to
speculative exigencies. Third Avenue stock
commacded as high as 4 per cent premium for
use on loans. There was furious manipulation
In the stock all day as was evidenced by the
clmultaneous sales on regular transactions and
un sellers flzty-day contracts at nearly 10
points apart, the regular sale commanding the
fcigh price. Other Â«tocks in which the short

except in Southern California, - Arizona and
along Puget Sound, where It Is cloudy and
threater.ir.K- Light rain has fallen In Arizona

Jr. otiier district*.

Boston Wool Market.
BOSTON. March 20.â The wool market here
Is dull as a whole and sales are meager. Wool
buyers are holding oft and not disposed to bid
on large lots, consequently prices are nominal,
although there is jno doubt that a bid under
present quotations for a large line would bring
Territory wools are quiet and are yet quoted
on a scoured .basis of about 60c for choice lots
fine medium and fine. Fleece wools are quiet
and prices nominal.

London Wool Sales.
LONDON. March 20.â At the wool auction
sales to-day 14,346 bales were offered. There
was a good attendance and the catalogues were
the largest of the series and were principally
New Zealand wools. The withdrawals were
large. A good selection of merinos and lots
suitable for America were actively competed
for. the bulk of the greasy lambs going to the
home trade and crossbreds to Yorkshire and
American representatives.

NEW YORK. March 20.â Considerable irregu

$16 60. On the other hand tin ruled easier in
sympathy with weakness abroad, closing dull

37% points off from the previous close. Plglron'

London Market.
NEW YORK. March 20.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram, says:
There was considerable Increase In . the busi
ness In the markets here, although It was
mainly professional. Still, the public Is thought
to be waking up at last. The general , tone was
good. Consols were up on one closing of Dig
bear commitments and Paris buying. The war
loan sold at 2 3-16 premium and 4 per cant is
talked of. . - Â¦ . .
American were good all day. although not
brink. London bought Baltimore and Ohio and
Union Pacific, while New York supported the
rise which the Morgan stocks started.
Paris keeps Tlntos firm, but New Yory allows
Anacondas to droop. The ban? bought Â£62.000
gold in bars and lost Â£10,000 for an unstated
Washington call.
Money was easier. Less than half the amount
due the bank was repaid, but money was more
plentiful.

DRIED FRUITS.
There was very little doing in exaporated
apples to-day. The market, however, showed a
weak undertone most of the day on liberal of
ferings and larger receipts. But Influences In
the wa> of favorable country advices served to
check any decline and the market closed steady
at unchanged prices. A small Jobbing business
was In progress for California dried fruits at
old prices.

CHICAGO, March 20.â Early trading In wheat
was marked by considerable liquidation. Cables
were lower at the opening and continued to
decline, which, â¢ with the continued large re
ceipts in the Northwest and the absence of
foreign demand, left the market practically
without support. May opened unchanged at
C6%@66>4c. Pressure to liquidate, which soon
became quite general, forced the price back
to 66Vic. Below this point, however, the bears
could not force the market and shortly before
11 o'clock a good demand sprang up, local
shorts and outsiders giving support to the mar
ket and advancing the price to 66% c. This
demand satisfied the market again relapsed
into a condition of heaviness with the price
fluctuating within a narrow range. During the
last hour of trading strength again developed.
The visible decreased 1,328,000 bushels, which
was more than expected. This stiffened the
market appreciably and when the seaboard
reported an Improved foreign demand the mar
ket became quite brisk. May reaching the
best point of the day. 66% c, at which, price
the market closed eteady.
Corn was dull and inclined to weakness,
prices ruling a little lower. News was such
as not to materially Influence prices and the
market was controlled by scalpers. May closed
Â»ie lower.
Oats showed no indications of recovering
from Its present stagnation. Speculative busi
ness was Insignificant. May closed a shade
lower.
Provisions were active, the market at times
being In a semi-panicky condition so far as
pork was concerned and the price of that
commodity covered a wide range. Small stocks
of pork, the continued advance in the price of
hops and fear that a corner might be at
tempted brought about a strong buying move
ment shortly after the opening and as the
price went skyward shorts tumbled over them
selves In their eagerness to cover. The price
advanced 50c In a very few minutes. Their
offerings became liberal and the market fell
a great deal faster than it had advanced, 1 20 c
being clipped off quickly. Another flurry
chopped off 30c more, but before the close con
fidence had been restored and the price again
advanced to about yesterday's closing figures.
Late in the session there was ' considerable
selling put on lard and ribs and those com
modities suffered in consequence, pork also
weakening somewhat. The close was rather
nervous with May pork 2%c lower, May lard
7%c lower and May ribs 7V4c lower.
The leading futures ranged as follows:

Quit Drug Coffee and Uses Postum
Cereal Coffee.
"While on a visit to a relative in New
Hampshire, who runs a fruit farm, I
found on the supper table what appeared
to be a strong cup of coffee. I usually
drank tea at night and they knew it, but
asked me to try the coffee, saying it was
Postu:n Food Coffee, and the reason
they served it was that they found when
they got up early in the summer morn-
ings and drank a cup of coffee to brace
up on they generally had a headache or
tick stomach along about noon, and that
their grocer persuaded them some time
before my visit to try Postum Food
Coffee. After a week's trial they adopt-
ed' it for the family, and had never re-
turned to the ordinary coffee, saying
that it had worked a wonderful change
in all of them â no more sick headaches,
no more sick stomachs.
"For two weeks I used Postum, and
when I returned to Boston I banished
tea and coffee from the table forever.
My complexion has made a decided
change for the better, and it poes with-
out saying that I feel greatly benefited.
My njghts are not sleepless now, as they
were when I was a coffee drinker.
M. E. Curtis, Canterbury St., Boston-
Mass."
COACH KELLY.
BERKELEY, March 20.â Addison W.-
Kelly, familiarly known as "King"
Kelly, has accepted the University
of California's offer to coach the
football squad next term and will assume
his. duties about September 16. Kelly is
the best available man and as he was
assistant coach last year to Garrett Coch
ran he is thoroughly familiar with the
conditions at Berkeley. The football men
were very anxious that Kelly accept the
offer and as he has the confidence of .the
student body the football outlook is very
bright. "Â¦Â¦ ' Â¦Â¦Â¦Â¦Â¦Â¦.-Â¦'Â¦ . -
W. A.' Shuey, f potball manager, who
was'reported to have resigned, has writ-,
ten a communication to the. students
stating that if a graduate manager is to
be installed It should be done Immediately
and in that case he will resign. \ â¢
BEADY TO JUMP
AND SPRINT ON
BERKELEY OVAL
Athletes of the Academic
League Expect to Lower
Records.
For the sixth year the young athletes
of the Academic League will meet on Sat
urday for their spring field day. The
grounds of the University of California at
Berkeley will be the scene of the meeting
and a new series of league records will
probably be established. The general re
vival In sports of the field is indicated by

Vedantic Philosophy.
6 warn! Vivekananda, the Hindoo ex
ponent of the Vedantic school; of philos

series of lectures upon the elements of the

POSTUM CEKEAX.
Speedway Horsemen Meet.
The members of the Golden Gate Park
Driving Association met last night and
perfected arrangements for the first races
of the Eeason on Saturday .over the park
speedway. Superintendent McLaren has
promised that the track will be in first
class condition for the occasion. This is
the first of a. series of bi-monthly races
which will culminate in the big open
meeting at Oakland track on Decoration
day. It is expected that these matinee
races will prepare both the horses and
their drivers for the warmer competition
which the open meeting will develop.
E. Hinkel, J. M. Eva and Leo Nolan
were elected members of the association
last night

ers in Chinatown.
Detective Gibson, in company with Ser
geant Patrick Mahoney and the high
binder sQuad, made one of the best round
ups of Chinese murderers which has been
made In this city for some time. Four
men, who will be tried each on several
charges of murder, a highbinder with a
gun and several more who will be booked
later for capital offenses was ' yesterday's
record. . - Â¦ /
The officers recently gained information
that a number of Suey Sing hatchetmen
were about to return to this city .to com
mence operations. They set a trap for
them and last night the following were
arrested: Lou Fook, wanted for a murder
in the Jackson street theater; Sue Ho
Mon. wanted for the killing of three men;
Ho Mun. wanted for the recent murder
of Wong Yuen on Waverley place, and
Jung Sing, supposed to have- been Impli
cated in a killing In a theater. In addition
to these captures Chung I Sing and Gon
Soom, notorious highbinders, on whose
heads crime may be fixed later, were also
taken.
All the men belong to the Suey Sing
Tong and : this society will be badly
crippled as the arrests mean the taking
away of some Â¦of its . choicest lawless
talent. Witnesses have promised the po
lice to assist in the ' prosecution of the
men and the hired assassins may yet get
their just deserts.
The highbinder war is still far from a
settlement and the merchants speak hope
lessly of bringing about an amicable ad
justment until more lives have been,sac
rificed. That the taking of blood is still
In vogue Is â¢ evinced by > the fact ' that

ACCUSED BY HEALY
Mechanics' Institute Property Will
Be Improved on Lines of Sugges
tion Made by Old Board.
The first meeting of the new board of
directors of the Mechanics' Institute was
held last evening. The recommendation
that the Pavilion and library property be
improved, as madeby the old board, was
favorably acted upon, and a committee
consisting of Messrs. Irving, Plum, Healy,
Taussig and Wallace, appointed to take
up the matter.
During the progress of the meeting.
Director Healy preferred charges against
Librarian Teggart for general incompet
ency. The charges were simply a reitera
tion of those made at previous times by
the director, and were referred to a com
mittee consisting of Directors Plum,
Meade and Taussig for consideration.
