ENDS HER LIFE BECAUSE
OF HUSBAND'S TARDINESS
Joseph Zddy of Chico Is Late to His
Dinner and His Wife Shoots
Herself.
Fperlal Dispatch to The Call.
CHICO. March 20. â Because her husband
was late to dinner last evening Mrs.
Joseph Eddy placed the muzzle of a 44
caliber revolver against her body and
pulled the trigger. The bullet tore its
way through the lower part of the abdo
men, coming out at a point just above
the left hip bone. So close was " the
weapon held that the woman's clothing
was s et on fire and only tho timely arrival
of a woman in the house prevented. Ijcr
being cremated. Mrs. Eddy stated that
her first Intention was to kill her husband
also, but on seeing him she changed her
mind. Little hope is entertained for her
recovery.
DAMROSCH LEADS
THE ORCHESTRA
ANEW leaf was added to the musi
cal history of , San Francisco yes
terday afternoon by the concert un
der the leadership of Mr. Walter
Damrosch, which took place at the Grand
Opera-house. The audience. In spite of
the Lenten season, was the largest that
has ever attended such an ' affair in this
city, and it was as generous in applause
as it was In numbers.
The elements of attraction were mani
fold. We were to hearMme. Gadskl and
Mr. Blspham again; ther* was the prom
ise of selections from Wagner's operas,
with competent orchestral accompani
ment; and an instrumental programme of
more than ordinary Interest; but the
most potent factor in drawing the people
was, without doubt, the fact that Mr.
Damroech, whose personality has capti
vated every one, was to conduct, and the
faith that there waa in his ability was,
fully confirmed. Â¦Â¦..â¢'
Mr. Damrosch is not an absolutely com
pelling force as a leader of orchestra; he
Is not so much the titan as the wizard;
he does not command respect, yet wins it.
Despite meager rehearsing he succeeded
in puting his band In good shape and his
work must be considered a creditable
achievement. The orchestral numbers
were given with cleanness and precision,
strength and an abundant infusion of
spirit; above all â¢ there was rhythm and
flow, which speaks not only for a native
quality In the conductor, but for his quite
remarkable faculty for training his men
to his own sense of the requirements. '
The Tannhauser overture and the Vor- :
eplel of Lohengrin were given readings
such as they have never had in San Fran
cisco. It is not for every leader to attain
to the true Wagnerian 6plrlt and those
who have shown the greatest powers in
the treatment of other composers have
frequently fallen short in their Interpre
tation of Wagner.
Mr. Damrosch showed his mastery par
ticularly in these items through the two
movements of Beethoven's Fifth Sympho
ny, the fourteenth Liszt Rhapsodic and
Tschaiskowsky's "Marche Slave," in
which he was not merely satisfying, but
brilliant. The orchestral element of the
programme was rich with color and ef
fectiveness and the admirable security
and delicious rhythm which the band bor
rowed from the clever conductor will not
soon be forgotten.
Mme. Gadski's singing: of Weber's
"Ocean, Thou Mighty Monster," was pos
itively inspiring and when she sang for
encore "The Valkyrie's Cry" she gained
applause such as one seldom hears. Mr.
Bispham was at his best in excerpts from
"Tannhauser," a song -by Brahms, Schu
bert's "Serenade" and the Widmung of
Schumann. Mr. Damrosch's ! accompani
ments of the last two items were most
musicianly. The impression that Mr. Bls
pham has made was attested by a suc
cession of recalls, which were as sincere
as they were vociferous and well, de
served.
We owe a debt of gratitude to the com
mittee who had the recent symphony sea
son in charge and through /vhose Instru
mentality this concert waa arranged and
If our last hearing of these delightful ar
tists was a pleasure we take leave of
them with the keenest regret. They have
done much for the uplifting of musical
taste in San Francisco and it is to be
hoped they have paved the way for a
future visitation.

ADMIRAL DEWEY TOO
ILL TO PARTICIPATE
Savannah's Eeception in His Honor
) Delayed Because of an Attack .
of Indigestion.
SAVANNAH, Ga., March 20.â The pro
gramme for . the entertainment of Ad
miral and Mrs. . Dewey was broken this
morning by the illness of the admiral. A
public reception had been -arranged for
the hours between J> and 11 o'clock and
5000 persons gathered at the De Soto Ho
tel to greet the distinguished visitors.
But the admiral' was too ill to appear and
thus the function had to be called off.
The admiral's attack, which is of indi
gestion, occurred- on board the revenue
cutter Hamilton while the party was on
a trip down the river and harbor. Sur
geon General -Wyman of the Marine Hos
pital service and Dr. St. J.- B. Graham,
Who "were aboard the cutter, administered
to the; admiral, but his indisposition was
so severÂ© that it was deemed best to bring
him back to the city. The admiral is rest
ing easily to-night and his physicians say
he will no doubt be able to go on with the
programme of arrangements to-morrow.
All the shipping in the harbor, foreign
and domestic, was decorated gayly in
honor of ' the admiral. Mrs. Dewey â¢ was
entertained this afternoon by a committee
of ladies at the yacht club, where she
heard . plantation songs by a chorus of
pickannlnies. . . _

annoying his ex-employer.
"If young children are trained in a kindergarten," quoth he,
"why not baby ducks?"

incubator and set about the task of rearing a' feathered fam

SUBSIDY BILL WILL
BENEFIT PACIFIC COAST
Special Dtayatcb to The OUL
. TACOMA, March 20.â Hugh C. Wallace,
president of the Washington and Alaska
Steamship Company, has returned from a
four months' trip to England and Wash
ington, Â£>. C. It is understood that his
time while away was devoted largely to
negotiations looking toward the organiza
tion of another and larger steamship com
pany, which will absorb the business of
the Northern Pacific Steamship Com
pany and North American Mall
Steamship Company, now operating
out of this port. Mr. Wallace
devoted his energies while at Washington
to the Interests of the latest ship subsidy
. bill, which, as now agreed upon. Is ex
pected to pass Congress very soon. Thia
bill will appropriate J20.000.000 for ship
subsidies on the Atlantic and Pacific
coasts. Atlantic coast shipping interests
endeavored to so arrange matters that the
entire, amount would be expended on ser
vices out of Atlantic ports. To combat
this proposition the interests of thÂ© Pa
cific Mall Steamship Company. Northern
Pacific Steamship Company and Great
Northern Railway were united in their
defense. The result of their efforts vas
to secure an arrangement whereby 30 per
cent of the total amount, or J0.000.000 an
nually, shall be devoted to Pacific Coast
commerce-It will follow that this amount
will be divided somewhat in proportion to
the number of their steamships between
thÂ© three Pacific Coast companies named.
The bill also provides that British steam
era can participate in subsidy benefits
providing the company operating them
guarantees to expend a certain amount
In the building of American steamships.
Thia Indicates that the Great Northern*
new steamship line out of Seattle may
consist first of British steamers, to be fol
lowed later by American built boats.

CUBANS NOT
VET READY FOR
INDEPENDENCE
Secretary of State Root's
Observations on the
Islands. '
WASHINGTON, March 20.â Secretary
Root this afternoon gave to President Mc-
Kinley the results of his observations of
affairs in Cuba during his recent trip to
the island. Subsequently the Secretary
consented to talk for publication as to ex
isting conditions in the island and the pos
sibilities of the people in the direction of?
self-government.
The Secretary expressed himself as Â«at-
Isheu on the whole with what he saw.
Naturally the question of the ultimate in
dcr>Â«rdence of the island came up for dis
cussion during the Secretary's visU to
Cuba, but he was not prepared to say
Just when the United States Government
would be willing that this should b-Â» a
reality.
Incidental to this matter, which is up
permost in the Cuban mind, are the steps
that will bring it about. The first of these,
viz., the holding of the municipal elections
at an early date, was a subject that Sec
retary Hoot discussed freely, particularly
with the United States officials on the ls
and.
"These elections." said the Secretary,
"will be the Cubans' first effort toward
popular government. Unaccustomed as
they are to these things, they are aa th<Â»
b.ihe unborn and have everything to
learn. The whole plan for making effect
ive the scheme of municipal suffrage has)
been very carefully worked out and the.
people instructed in alt the details. This
involves a vast amount of labor and takes
considerable- time, but ultimately the plan
will be worked out and the elections held.
I hardly believe, however, that the author
ities will be- quite ready for holding the
elections by the Ist of May.

eaid estate.

COAL COMPANY IS
BLAMED BY A JURY
Death of Edward Fitzgerald Said to
. Have Been Due to Lack of
. j ' Precaution. Â¦
" Edward Fitzgerald, a coal-passer, resid
ing at.l2o6Folsom street, had the misfor
tune to be killed on March. l 3 by falling off
a runway on the coal bunkers of the Pa
cific Coast Railway. - The evidence taken
at ' the' inquest -.before Coroner Cole yes
terday i morning showed that if the run
Â¦way had " been constructed with proper
safeguards the accident â¢ might not have
happened. . . Â» - ,
;The jury took this view of the' case and
returned a verdict, to the effect. that tht
deceased had come ;; to his Â¦ death from
shock due to Â«f racture of the skull . and
tho verdict goes on to : say : "From ! fall
ling off ; a. run way' on the coal bunkers of
[the Pacific Coast Company. while. in an in
toxicated > condition, and we strongly rec-.
ommend, in order; to prevent. accidents of
a similar nature in the future,' that in all
, places where ,the Pacific Coast Company
uses movable wire rope fences a perma
nent, bumping; block not less -than 'eight
inches high should be placed on the edgt>
of the runway. ; -
Â¦', "And we further recommend ? that the
posts supporting the wire rope should be
of a more substantial character and more
firmly attached -to the surface of the run
way, and that the Pacific Coast Company
should jirovlde . better lighting facilities..
The -Coroner approved of ,Â¦ the . verdict.
GENERAL SHAFTER WILL
PRESENT THE CANNON
Ceremonies in Front of the City Hall
at Noon To-Day, When the Old
Gun Will Be Put on Guard.
To-day at 12:30 o'clock General Shatter
will present to Mayor Phelan, as repre
senting the city, the old twenty-four
liounder that was captured at Santiago.
The ceremonies will not be elaborate, but
their military dignity will be enhanced by
the attendance of General Shafter's staff
Jn full ares*, two troops of the Sixth Cav
ulry and Ihe band of the Third Arjlllery.
General Shatter will make the presen
tation adcress: and Mayor Pholan will ac
cept the gun. It will be mounted in front
of the group of statuary on the Market
etreet side.
The gun is a curiosity ln more ways
than one. Its inscriptions show it to be
of high and ancient lineage, made by
the best founder of its time and a cannon
used to good company and plenty of ac
tive service. Its grim old chase is in
scribed with the words "Le Prince de
Conde" and then the significant motto,
"Ultima Ratio Regnum" ("The Last Ar
gument of Kings"). This was originally a
Spanish motto, which form, "Ultima.
Kazon de Reyes," was used frequently on
the Spanish ordnance. Louis XIV of
France saw the beauty of the sentiment
and ha ordered it, rendered into Latin, to
be inscribed on all the French guns of that
period. " "
Farther down on the chase are several
names, perhaps of the commanders under
whom the old gun tought, perhaps of sol
diers whose deeds were gallant enough to
warrant their names being preserved on
the old bronze warrior. The names are:
Louis Charles de Bourbon, Comte d'Eu
and Due d'Aumale. It is certain, how
t-vcr, that they have no connection with
the name of the gun. for by its position
Oor.de's name seems to have that honor.
Ar.d tbe last of all comes another motto,
"No Pluribus Impair" ("No unequal an
tagonist tor many"), which well reflects
the ppirit cast in the metal of the cannon.
It was a big: piece of ordnance in its day,
although now it would have to give the
â¢wall to a one-pounder.

GUARDED HIS WIFE
WITH A SHOTGUN
A Bride of Six Weeks Makes Sensa-
tional Charges Against Her
* Young Husband.
After six weeks of wedded "bliss" Mrs.
Josephine C. Cobb of 1413 Van Xess ave
nue has decided that longer residence
with her husband, Harry D. M. Cobb. a
clerk In the employ of the Pacific Mail
Steamship Company, would be extremely
unsafe, hence she sued him for divorce
on the ground of cruelty. , In her com
plaint Mrs. Cobb alleges that on the even
ing of February 8 last she forsook her
maiden name of Egan and became Mrs.
Cobb. After a brief wedding; tour the
young couple icturned ; to this city and
took up their residence at 1413 Van Ness
Just seventeen days of the honeymoon
had passed, she says, when her husband
pointed a loaded shotgun at her head. Two
days later, according to the complaint, he
choked her, and on March 5 locked her ln
her room and fctood guard at the dooi
with a loaded shotgun. She retreated be
hind a wardrobe and there remained until
her husband's anger cooled off. Mrs.
Cobb. in addition to a decree of divorce,
asks that her husband be compelled to re
turn her $500 she loaned him and pay her
permanent alimony in the sum of ?tt a
month. .
do shook the witness' testimony in this
regard. . Â¦
Secretary Rock of the Grand Jury, suc
ceeded Mrs. Gilleran on the stand. "Have
you any letter, Mr. Rock, purporting to
be from Mr. Dunand to myself?" began
Lafe Pence. Under objection the letter
was produced and read into the record.
It was nothing more or less than a letter
apprising Pence of Dunand's itinerary on
his Mexican and Central American trip.
Rock admitted that he had no other paper
signed by Dunand, Mrs. Craven-Fair or
Henry Koehler Jr. '
"In whose hands was the $30,000 contract
between Mrs. Craven-Fair and Mr. Du
nand when you . received it?" inquired
Lafe Pence.
To this McEnerney objected, provoking
a long speech from Lafe Pence, who de
clared that it was necessary and import
ant to know in whose . hands the paper
had been. The clock at this stage regis
tered adjourning time.
There was rather a full house for the
afternoon session, which did not last long
enough to satisfy a tithe of the idle curi
osity manifested In the case. Mrs. Craven-
Fair was expected to take the stand, but
again that lady remained away and the at
torneys for the Fair heirs were forced to
content themselves with a few interroga
tory shafts directed against the much
abused Mr. E. R. Rock. . . Â¦
Hardly had Judge Troutt taken his seat
when Lai' e . Pence arose and asked the
court to admit in evidence the famous
"Do you know your name" slip. Of
course, McEnerney battled against It,
claiming that it was not yet proved to
be in the handwriting, of James Q. Fair,
Tho court willed otherwise and the slip
was admitted.
Then Rock was called for the second
time during the day. The Pence brother
hood did not want much, but they wanted
that very badly. "Was the lead pencil
indorsement of the Dunand-Craven con
tract on it when it came to your hands?"
was Lafe Pence's inquiry. .With an ap
peal to the court before replying, and re
ceiving an answer from Judge Troutt to do
so, Rock, said that it was. Further ex
amination brought out the fact that the
Grand Jury did not have any other papers
with Mrs. Craven-Fair's signature- than
the contract to which she and Dunand had
subscribed their names. . The Simpton af
fidavit, called the "midnight affidavit,"
was produced by Mr. Rock on the request
of Attorney Pierson and read oft for the
benefit of the Pence brotherhood as well
as the phalanx of Fair attorneys.
Here the case came to its sudden and
unexpected adjournment. Judge Troutt
readily agreeing to the wishes of the at

The Assault on Barling.
The preliminary examination of Â¦P. Â¦-. J.
Curtin of Curtin's detective agency on the
charge of an assault with Intent to do
grievous bodily harm upon H. J." Barling,
a friend of Mrs. Craven,' was begun. befora
Judge Cabaniss yesterday afternoon. .
Mrs. Mary Ruggles, 812 Post street, tes
tified that she had gone with Barling to
Judge Troutt's courtroom to listen to the
testimony in the Craven-Fair case. -After
they left the courtroom two men attacked
Barling. They knocked him down and
beat him with their, fists and feet. She
was too excited to take particular notice
of the men.
Barling testified that he was. a salmon
packer. He recognized the defendant as a
man who had been following him around
for about two months prior to the assault
in. the corridor of the City Hall; He de
scribed how the defendant and the other
man attacked and kicked him on the face
and body while he was down. Â¦
E. W. Schmidt, an oil man from San
Diego, testified that he heard a . woman
scream and saw. a fight, but he* could not
identify the defendant. Theodore Kytka
also saw the fiay and heard the woman
scream. Ex-Chief Lees testified to lifting
Barling up after the fight and as to the
condition of his face.' The case was con
tinued till Friday. - %

SCHOOL BOARD STRIKES
BACK AT R.H. WEBSTER
Chairman Mark Replies to Criticisms
on the Methods of the ;*;AÂ«i
Directors. . . I
The Board of Education yesterday,
struck back at School Superintendent
Webster, who, for some time past, has
given expression to some severe strictures
regarding the policy of that body onva-:
rious matters affecting the welfare. of the'
public schools. President Mark presented,
an open letter, in which he says that his
attention has been called: to interviews
that have been published attacking " the
board for its method of conducting the
aflairs of the department. .'" â¢
"The Superintendent has seen fit,". says
Mr Mark, "to criticize us for the recent
consolidations. His statement; that .we
have thereby impaired the efficiency,, of
the schools is false. We have made com
paratively few consolidations and only
where the average daily attendance was
far below forty-five pupils to a class. The
Superintendent further states that In our
financial reports we have purposely mis
led the public by claiming a saving in the
department to our â¢ management and not
giving credit to the last board. If I have
seemed at any time to -take away any.
credit to which it was entitled I desire to
apologize for so doing. I
"The Superintendent further contends
that we have destroyed- the 'merit sys
tem' and substituted a plan of handing
out positions to favorites. I presume by
'merit system* he means observing strictly
the chronological order of appointment.
Length of service does ' not necessarily
make for efficiency, but breadth of expe
rience should be -the watchward. In all
our recent consolidations not one experi
enced teacher has been placed on the sub
stitute list, but all ' have been assigned
Immediately to a class. The base insinua
tion that we are In league with thoÂ»prin
cipals for the sole purpose .of .breeding
out teachers is an Insult to the board; and
any one who dares to make such a state
ment basely "misrepresents the facts of the
case. â¢ Â¦?Â¦-,
'"I deny that the board Intends to reduce
salaries, bu# we are determined to Â¦ con
duct the department on strict business
principles. The fact that the Janitors and
employes ln the : repair - department ; will
be paid by the Board of Public Works will
reduce the salary roll over $55,000 a year.
Taklnj the Superintendent's estimate Â¦of

FISCHER SUCCEEDS IN
PLEASING THE PUBLIC
The New Concert House Opens TJnder
Auspices That Are More Than
Favorable. 1
Fischer's concert house at 122 O'Farrell
street, which opened for the first time on
Monday night, bids fair to become one of
the most popular places of amusement in
the entire city. /" ..
The capacity of the house is about 1000,
but such has been the rush the doors were
closed at a little after 8 o'clock last night,
and from that time until midnight the
doorkeepers had their hands full ln turn
ing away the disappointed ones who had
come too late to get a seat. :
The new concert hall Is beautifully deco
rated in white and gold, the acoustic* aie
excellent and the programme Â¦ that is
nightly rendered includes everything that
is best from ragtime to grand opera.
Taking it all in all the place is Just such
a one as the city has long wished lorâ a
place where the expense of admission is
small enough to allow of one dropping in
for an hour or so without feeling con
strained to remain during the whole pro
gramme in order to get his moneys worth.
The programmes rendered are the best
that musical talent <can produce, and the
regulations that govern the place make it
impossible for objectionable characters to
make their presence felt and give to the
house a reputation that might in time de
bar others from lending their patronage
to It.
The place will remain open every even
ing until midnight,, and matinees will be
given every Saturday and Sunday after
noon. It is the desire of Mr. Fischer to
give to the public a class of musical en
tertainment that will be attractive to fam
ilies, and to offer to the public a pro
gramme that may be enjoyed by the
wives and daughters of our nicest people.
Hence the matinees. :.:.;. , .;

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, MARCH 21, 1900.
SCAVENGERS HAD
NO TROUBLE IN
DODGING POLICE
Only One Man Was Attested
While Hundreds Were
fSgs&RZsm wiiniuioolCU
'Â¦Â¦Â¦Â¦â¢â¢.Â¦'Â¦Â¦Â¦ _Â¦ ?.--' >
Carts Passed Without Covers Through
th* Streets i and as Usual Dis- !
seminated the - Bacteria of
Di-ease Freely.
The police arrested one ' scavenger yes
terday for violating the -city ordinance,
which provides that it shall be unlawful
to use a cart for the collection and re
moval ..of general . refuse , unless j it
Â¦hall be perfectly tight and with a heavy
cover, to be approved by the Board of
Health, tightly fastened on at all UmM
when the owner or driver Is not collecting
or depositing the general refuse. How It
was that only one scavenger was arrested
does not appear. . All day there were un
covered scavenger carts, the drivers of
which were utterly ignoring the law, mov
ing about all over the city. There are over
200 licensed scavenger carts and all of
them were probably at ; work. There â¢ was
not a street through which they did not
pass at some hour of the day. The man
who I was taken in was C. Caesarevitch,
and he was arrested by Sergeant Tobln on
Powell street. â¢ - ...... ... .
Chief of ' Police Sullivan was asked yes
terday morning why no arrests were made
under the ordinance Â¦ when it was noto
rious that the scavengers were openly vio
lating it and had been doing so for a long
period. The Chief pulled down a copy of
the city ordinances and looked the matter
up, and at once gave an order for the ar
rest of violators of the ordinance in ques
tion. The order seemed to have little ter
rifying effect upon the law-breakers, for
some mysterious reason. Market street
and other principal streets -were crossed
and recrossed and traversed with impu
nity. 'Â¦: Not one scavenger cart in the city
engaged in collecting general refuse'yes
terday, or If ever, had a cover fastened to
it, as the ordinance directs. The nearest
approach to compliance was a' covering
of coarse cloth, partly over the load an-i
not attached to the cart, although tucked
under a part of the load to keep It from
blowing away. As a preventative against
the escape "of disease breeding bacteria It
was utterly and dangerously inadequate.
The ordinance provides for the approval
of the cover by the Board of Health after
Inspection, and no license can issue fur a
scavenger cart until after the Board of
Health has certified, through the proper
officer, that the ordinance has been com
plied with. Nevertheless there are in evl
dence over 200 licenses. The scavengers
have evidently bftlieved that the matter
could all be fixed up with the ~ officials,
otherwise they would not have ignored the
ordinance. A large body of citizens aro
determined that the loose methods of the
garbage collectors shall not be continued.
More developments are expected.
